class="css-6n7j50"><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="More sharing options" class="css-16ogagc" data-testid=""><svg class="css-uhuo44" height="13" viewbox="0 0 16 13" width="16"><path clip-rule="evenodd" d="M15.406 5.359L8.978 0v3.215C3.82 3.215.406 8.107.406 12.66 1.653 9.133 4.29 7.517 8.978 7.517v3.2l6.428-5.358z" fill="#000" fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-60hakz"></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></header></div><section class="meteredContent css-1i2y565" itemprop="articleBody" name="articleBody"><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Senator Kirsten Gillibrand was in a bind.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">With less than three weeks until the deadline to get into the fall presidential debates — which she deemed crucial to keeping her campaign alive —<span class="css-8l6xbc evw5hdy0"> </span>she was on track to fall well short. She had neither the 130,000 donors she needed nor the necessary support in the polls. What she did have was a stockpile of cash. So, in one Hail Mary heave, she unloaded $1.5 million on a two-week television buy in the doldrums of August to try to improve her numbers in Iowa and New Hampshire.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“The alternative to not going all in,” said Glen Caplin, a senior adviser to Ms. Gillibrand, “was not a viable alternative.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The gamble would prove to be a final miscalculation. If the commercials caused any discernible Gillibrand bump, it would go undetected: No Iowa or New Hampshire polls that could have qualified her were even conducted after her ads aired. On Wednesday, with the deadline just hours away, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/08/28/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-2020-drop-out.html?module=inline" title="">Ms. Gillibrand dropped out</a>.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“It’s important to know when it’s not your time,” Ms. Gillibrand <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/SenGillibrand/status/1166826680134057984" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">said in a video</a>.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">How Ms. Gillibrand, 52, so swiftly went from a rising star of the Democratic resistance and <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.cbsnews.com/news/senator-kirsten-gillibrand-the-metoo-senator/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">“the #MeToo senator,”</a> as “60 Minutes” had memorably tagged her in 2018, to a 2020 afterthought and early primary casualty is a tale of mistakes, misfortune and a message that did not meaningfully hold sway in a historically crowded field.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand, of New York, entered the race pitching herself as <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/02/12/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-president-feminist.html?module=inline" title="">the voice of feminism</a> and the defender of families and women’s equality. She championed a new “Family Bill of Rights,” pioneered a new litmus test to select only judges who supported Roe v. Wade and traveled to Republican-controlled states to protest new restrictions on abortion.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand2/merlin_153740571_d3050ed5-2aac-410c-8edf-9d925a0c424a-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><img alt="Of the six female candidates, Ms. Gillibrand was the first to call it quits, having found little traction among women or men." class="css-1m50asq" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand2/merlin_153740571_d3050ed5-2aac-410c-8edf-9d925a0c424a-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" itemprop="url" sizes="((min-width: 600px) and (max-width: 1004px)) 84vw, (min-width: 1005px) 60vw, 100vw" src="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand2/merlin_153740571_d3050ed5-2aac-410c-8edf-9d925a0c424a-articleLarge.jpg?quality=75&amp;auto=webp&amp;disable=upscale" srcset="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand2/merlin_153740571_d3050ed5-2aac-410c-8edf-9d925a0c424a-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 600w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand2/merlin_153740571_d3050ed5-2aac-410c-8edf-9d925a0c424a-jumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 1024w,https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand2/merlin_153740571_d3050ed5-2aac-410c-8edf-9d925a0c424a-superJumbo.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp 2048w"/></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Of the six female candidates, Ms. Gillibrand was the first to call it quits, having found little traction among women or men.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Maddie McGarvey for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But of the six female candidates, she was the first to call it quits, having found little traction among women or men. She almost never topped 1 percent in a poll. In fact, Ms. Gillibrand ended up as the female candidate with the fewest donors, trailing even the political newcomer Marianne Williamson, despite having spent years trying to build a national following.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0"><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">[Who’s in? Who’s out? </em><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/us/politics/2020-presidential-candidates.html?action=click&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Article" title=""><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">Keep up with the 2020 field with our candidate tracker</em></a><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">.]</em></p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“She was running in a lane of fighting on women’s issues, but other people were running in that lane as well,” Randi Weingarten, the president of the American Federation of Teachers, said. “You had several female candidates who were all bringing their own brand of feminism.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand said in an interview that she was unsure what the missing piece of her candidacy was. “I don’t know,” she said. “My campaign may well have been ahead of its time.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Little seemed <!-- -->to click this year.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Her kickoff event was overshadowed by the release of the first snippets of the Mueller report, an unforeseeable development. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Yet she was also prone to self-inflicted wounds. At one Boston fund-raiser, Ms. Gillibrand asked a group of women to contact people on their Christmas-card and school-parent lists to ask for $1 donations to help her make the debate, according to one attendee, who said she and others were appalled by the implication that the women did not have professional circles of their own.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">B<!-- -->y early August, <!-- -->the downward trajectory was plain to see. Senator Kamala Harris of California was motoring across Iowa in a leather-appointed bus decorated with her campaign logo. A second bus of reporters and aides trailed behind.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand rode in a small R.V. with her campaign sign slapped on the side; her husband was behind the wheel. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">She attracted scant crowds (Andrew Yang, a first-time candidate, outdrew her splashy weekend kickoff rally on a rainy work night this spring; both events were in Manhattan). She failed to secure significant endorsements (only one New York member of Congress backed her). </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">And she was plagued by questions about her past. Her prominent role as the first Democratic senator to call for Al Franken’s resignation dogged her throughout the race, with voters and reporters bringing it up and some Democratic donors denouncing <!-- -->her<!-- -->. And Ms. Gillibrand’s record included policy reversals like her past support of gun rights and her opposition to “amnesty for illegal immigrants” — making her a hard sell for progressives focused on purity and consistency. </p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Her exact ideological bearing could prove elusive. Was she the upstate congresswoman who flipped a heavily Republican seat more than a decade ago, or the liberal firebrand who voted down nearly every one of President Trump’s nominees?</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand5/merlin_156904464_468dd237-8005-42af-a1e3-de1ccaab2d95-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Ms. Gillibrand in a mock debate session in Troy, N.Y., in June.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Patrick Dodson for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“There’s a false debate in the party right now,” Ms. Gillibrand said <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/video/postlive/wplive/sen-kirsten-gillibrand-says-democratic-nominee-has-to-be-both-moderate-and-progressive/2019/08/19/7ad0de6c-c117-417d-ae90-5300460b6b86_video.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">in late August</a>. “Either you have to be an uber-progressive who can inspire the base, or you have to be a moderate who wins those red and purple areas. I believe you have to do both. And my candidacy is both.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">As Ms. Gillibrand’s campaign languished, she began plunging ever more money into Facebook ads, prospecting for donors with money-losing offers like giving away T-shirts for $1. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0"><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">[</em><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/politics?smid=rd%3Faction%3Dclick&amp;module=inline&amp;pgtype=Article" title=""><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">Sign up for our politics newsletter</em></a><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0"> and join the conversation around the 2020 presidential race.]</em></p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Overall, she spent $2.8 million on Facebook — more than $20 for every contributor to her campaign. She ended with fewer than the required 130,000 donors and less than $800,000 in campaign funds.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“The sky-high expectations for her candidacy were also themselves a liability,” Jon Reinish, a former aide to Ms. Gillibrand, said. “When she didn’t shoot to the top right away, the perception game became an albatross she couldn’t shake.”</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-688bbb4d">On defense, from the start</h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand’s candidacy did not get off to an auspicious start. She had secured a big booking on her first full day, a spot on Rachel Maddow’s highly rated MSNBC show. The show is typically a friendly space for Democrats, but Ms. Maddow grilled Ms. Gillibrand about her political “transformation.” “She had an A rating from the N.R.A.,” Ms. Maddow said. “She said she wanted to make English the official language of the United States.” </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">It was the first of many interviews consumed by questions about her record.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1bmqgre epkadsg3"><div class="css-13ft82o epkadsg0">More coverage of Kirsten Gillibrand’s campaign</div><div class="css-15g2oxy epkadsg2"><div class="css-4q4vfu e16ij5yr6"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/08/28/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-2020-drop-out.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article"><div class="css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4"><div class="css-11jd139 e16ij5yr2">Gillibrand Drops Out of 2020 Democratic Presidential Race</div><time class="css-o15hd4 e16638kd0" datetime="2019-08-28T17:30:17-04:00">Aug. 28, 2019</time></div><div class="css-77hcv e16ij5yr0"></div></a></div><div class="css-4q4vfu e16ij5yr6"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/02/12/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-president-feminist.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article"><div class="css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4"><div class="css-11jd139 e16ij5yr2">‘This Is My Space’: Kirsten Gillibrand’s Unabashedly Feminist Campaign</div><time class="css-o15hd4 e16638kd0" datetime="2019-02-12T05:00:11-05:00">Feb. 12, 2019</time></div><div class="css-77hcv e16ij5yr0"></div></a></div><div class="css-4q4vfu e16ij5yr6"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/06/24/us/politics/democratic-candidates-debates-preparation.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article"><div class="css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4"><div class="css-11jd139 e16ij5yr2">Stand-Ins, Push-Ups, Long Drives: How the 2020 Candidates Are Preparing for the Debates</div><time class="css-o15hd4 e16638kd0" datetime="2019-06-24T03:00:07-04:00">June 24, 2019</time></div><div class="css-77hcv e16ij5yr0"></div></a></div><div class="css-4q4vfu e16ij5yr6"><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/08/01/us/politics/2020-democratic-presidential-candidates.html?action=click&amp;module=RelatedLinks&amp;pgtype=Article"><div class="css-i9gxme e16ij5yr4"><div class="css-11jd139 e16ij5yr2">When Will the 2020 Democratic Field Start to Shrink?</div><time class="css-o15hd4 e16638kd0" datetime="2019-08-01T16:09:58-04:00">Aug. 1, 2019</time></div><div class="css-77hcv e16ij5yr0"></div></a></div></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">She became so adroit at apologizing for her past positions — to her disadvantage, guns and immigration developed into two prominent issues in the Democratic primary contest — that she tried to turn that skill into an asset, comparing it favorably to Mr. Trump’s lack of contrition. But she yielded no ground on Mr. Franken, saying he was not entitled to her “silence” after multiple, credible accusations of sexual misconduct. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“She really did carve a path for unapologetic feminism,” Ilyse Hogue, the president of the abortion-rights group NARAL, said. But, she added, “those who are fiercest and who choose to go toe-to-toe with entrenched misogyny are rarely rewarded.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Hogue said that the Franken episode had at times “overshadowed her campaign,” but she predicted that “history will show what she was doing was for the betterment of the country.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand’s campaign made <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/01/19/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-iowa-president.html?module=inline" title="">an early bet that the senator could slowly win over small audiences in Iowa</a> and New Hampshire living rooms. Her $10 million Senate war chest gave her a financial cushion to pursue the strategy. <!-- -->But the early <!-- -->months of the 2020 race showed the national narrative playing out on social media and television, not in coffee shops. That dynamic drove the early states, not the other way around.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand3/merlin_152568534_d54547b4-c320-4525-a038-95ff55e6c2df-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Ms. Gillibrand announced her presidential campaign in March.</span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Christopher  Lee for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“I didn’t see her show up like I know she could have and know that she can,” L. Joy Williams, the president of the Brooklyn N.A.A.C.P., said. “I don’t know why, to be honest with you.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Her campaign also failed to line up key supporters in New York, such as party officials and members of Congress. One planned dinner for lawmakers at her Washington home was canceled. Some New York donors said they wrote her a check out of obligation but<!-- --> declined to host a fund-raiser.<!-- --> Charlie King, a Democratic National Committee member from New York who is unaligned in the 2020 race, said he never heard from Ms. Gillibrand or her team.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“I heard from several other campaigns,” Mr. King said. “Multiple times.”</p><h2 class="css-ani50b eoo0vm40" id="link-5b20c4a">Money woes overshadow bright spots</h2><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand’s lack of small-donor support — the lifeblood of Democratic fund-raising — was evident from the start. Through June, she had topped 2,500 donations in a day only once; Senator Elizabeth Warren of Massachusetts had more than 100 such days.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In May, Ms. Gillibrand’s campaign began to <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/17/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-abortion.html?module=inline" title="">sever ties with Anne Lewis Strategies</a>, the political firm where she had directed $5.6 million in 2017 and 2018, in part to build a digital supporter list. Not enough of those people became 2020 donors. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Months into her bid, Ms. Gillibrand was hemorrhaging money. In the second quarter she spent nearly $2 million more than she raised — by far the worst ratio of any candidate who was not self-funding a run. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Rival campaigns took note. Some began privately discussing how soon would be too soon to try to poach some of the talent that Ms. Gillibrand had assembled in her Troy, N.Y., headquarters.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand had some bright spots. She sipped whiskey with voters, dressed up with drag queens in Des Moines, took spin classes everywhere and arm-wrestled with an Iowa college student. Her team quickly packaged these vignettes into videos posted online.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">During her Fox News forum in early June, she was dismissed by the moderator, Chris Wallace, as “not very polite” — a rare viral moment. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“We want women to have a seat at the table,” Ms. Gillibrand said. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“What about men?” Mr. Wallace asked. </p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“They’re already there — do you not know?” she replied.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In July, her answer <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/SenGillibrand/status/1149498925339910146" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">defining white privilege</a> ricocheted across the internet and drew plaudits from progressives. But by then the press corps trailing her had thinned. Voters seemed to have settled on a top tier of contenders. It did not include her.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-79elbk" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper"><div class="css-z3e15g" data-testid="photoviewer-wrapper-hidden"></div><div class="css-1a48zt4 ehw59r15" data-testid="photoviewer-children"><figure aria-label="media" class="css-jcw7oy e1g7ppur0" itemid="https://static01.nyt.com/images/2019/08/29/us/politics/29gillibrand4/merlin_158710488_9a833340-8ee5-47e2-8add-94c67c28622f-articleLarge.jpg?quality=90&amp;auto=webp" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/ImageObject" role="group"><div class="css-1xdhyk6 erfvjey0"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Image</span><div class="css-zjzyr8"><div data-testid="lazyimage-container" style="height:257.77777777777777px"></div></div></div><figcaption class="css-1l44abu e1xdpqjp0" itemprop="caption description"><span aria-hidden="true" class="css-8i9d0s e13ogyst0">Ms. Gillibrand and former Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr. during the second Democratic presidential debate. </span><span class="css-vuqh7u e1z0qqy90" itemprop="copyrightHolder"><span class="css-1ly73wi e1tej78p0">Credit</span><span>Maddie McGarvey for The New York Times</span></span></figcaption></figure></div></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand and her campaign knew the second debate was her last best shot to break through. And they thought they had found a perfect issue to take on the front-runner, former Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr.: his opposition to a child tax credit in 1980.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But Ms. Gillibrand <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/07/26/us/politics/kirsten-gillibrand-2020.html?module=inline" title="">telegraphed the attack</a> days before the debate.<!-- --> So Mr. Biden came prepared for her premeditated broadside, dismissing her questions about his record on defending women as expediency borne of the fact that she was now running against him. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Ms. Gillibrand received her first 2 percent qualifying poll soon afterward, which helped inspire the ill-fated ad blitz. Her advisers hoped better polling would inspire more donors to chip in.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In recent weeks, her campaign<!-- --> filled inboxes with pleas for cash as often as three times a day. Gloria Steinem signed emails. T-shirts were offered for $1.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“At this point, it’s now or never,” her campaign pleaded on Monday.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">It would be her last day on the trail. She took a spin class near San Francisco, canceled fund-raisers in Southern California and then flew to New York to huddle with her family on Tuesday evening.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“I came home, talked to my husband, my two boys, we had a very, very thoughtful and wonderful conversation about what the role of public service is,” she said, “and that mommy is dedicated to serving others, no matter what or in whatever role it is.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">On Wednesday, she broke the news to her headquarters staff in person. They ended the night together at a bar, drinking whiskey.</p><p class="css-jwz2nf etfikam0"><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">Alex Burns contributed reporting </em></p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div></section><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="css-1ubp8k9"></div><div class="css-wg1cha"><div class="css-19hdyf3 e1e7j8ap0"><div><p>Shane Goldmacher is a national political reporter and was previously the chief political correspondent for the Metro Desk. Before joining The Times, he worked at Politico, where he covered national Republican politics and the 2016 presidential campaign. <span class="css-4w91ra"> <a class="css-1rj8to8" href="https://twitter.com/ShaneGoldmacher" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank"><span class="css-0">@</span>ShaneGoldmacher</a> </span></p></div></div></div><div class="css-vdv0al">A version of
